QUESTIONS TO THE COMMISSION
Question no 38 by Liam Hyland (H-0463/03)
 Subject: The future of the wool market
 

Will the Commission outline what its plans are to develop and sustain the wool market in the European Union and outside the EU, and what future does it see for young farmers working in or wishing to work in this sector?

 
  
 

(EN) Before replying to the question of the honourable Member in more detail, the Commission would like to ask attention for the fact that it supports the incomes of the sheep farmer through a Common Market Organisation. There is no specific scheme at Community level to support the wool sector, and the Commission does not have plans to set up such a scheme in the foreseeable future. However, there are certain possibilities for support in this sector.

In this context the Commission would like to mention the rural development programmes, and more specifically the LEADER+ initiative, which have financed some projects concerning wool. The structural funds have also supported regional development projects of which the wool sector has been a beneficiary.

Concerning young farmers, with the common agriculture policy (CAP) reform agreed by the Council of Agriculture in June 2003, three new provisions in the Rural Development Regulation 1257/99 are to be introduced, which benefit young farmers:

Firstly, a new recital that establishes that young farmers should be considered a priority;

Secondly, an increase by 5 percentage points in maximum public support for investments in agricultural holdings. This means actually an increase from 45% to 50% in the non-less favoured areas and from 55% to 60% in less favoured areas, and

Thirdly, a higher support for the setting-up of young farmers, who are using farm advisory services linked to the setting-up, in this case the maximum premium increases from € 25 000 to € 30 000.

Once the legal texts of the CAP reform are adopted, Member States can make use of these new possibilities and adapt their rural development programmes. If there are young farmers who see opportunities in the wool sector and intend to set up a business and invest in it, these new provisions can of course benefit them. However, it is the young farmer as a market player who has to take these decisions based on the assessment of the business opportunities he/she sees.

 

Question no 39 by Seán Ó Neachtain (H-0464/03)
 Subject: Developing the commercial potential of our seaweed resources
 

Is the Commission satisfied that the full potential of our natural seaweed resources is being realised? In Ireland it only generates about €15 million annually but there is potential for much greater development. Among its many uses seaweed can be harvested for use in foods and food supplements, fertilisers, soil conditioners and feed for the aquaculture sector.

Will the Commission make a statement on the support it can provide for developing our seaweed resources, including research and training, and will it state what future proposals it has, if any, for this sector which can generate valuable new jobs in the coastal regions of the European Union and, furthermore, has it carried out studies on this sector in other EU Member States and outside the EU?

 
  
 

(EN) As pointed out by the honourable Member, seaweed represents a valuable asset that can contribute to the socio-economic development of coastal regions either through the harvesting of seaweed growing naturally or through active farming thereof. The former offers few possibilities for development in the Union as wild seaweed is already harvested to its full potential in most Member States. Actually, these Member States have developed complex legislation to make the best of the harvesting of wild seaweed in their near shore areas and to avoid the over exploitation of these sessile marine living resources.

As for seaweed farming, marginal quantities of seaweed are already farmed in the Union and potential for future expansion may exist in a number of Member States, as indicated in the recent Communication from the Commission to the Council and the Parliament 'A Strategy for the Sustainable Development of Aquaculture'(1). In this strategic document, the Commission recommends that aquaculture activities should be diversified and that more efforts should be dedicated to research on new species, especially species, such as seaweed, that are able to utilise primary production efficiently. In this respect, Community support has already been provided to at least two research projects aiming at the improvement of seaweed cultivation techniques, one for Laminaria digitata (project AIR3-CT94-2235), the other for the edible red seaweed Palmaria palmata (project FAIR-CT97-3828).

Member States wishing to develop seaweed farming may use European structural funds for this purpose. They may in particular dedicate a significant share of the funds allocated to them through the Financial Instrument for Fisheries Guidance (FIFG) to the development of aquaculture and/or to processing or marketing of its products. They may also follow the recommendation from the Commission to allocate greater public support to aquaculture diversification which could provide opportunities for future seaweed farming development.

 
 

(1) COM (2002)511 final

 

Question no 40 by María Rodríguez Ramos (H-0480/03)
 Subject: Reclassification of rural areas in the EU
 

In the light of the conclusions of the Court of Auditors' special report No 4/2003 and the critical remarks contained therein regarding the effectiveness of the support measures for less-favoured areas under the various rural development programmes, it is clear that the reform of the CAP will have the effect of increasing the risks of cessation of farming and depopulation in those areas of the Union.

Does the Commission intend to undertake a reclassification of rural areas, on the basis of objective criteria relating to the environment, employment and rurality, as called for by Parliament in its reports A5-0164/2002 on rural development in the framework of Agenda 2000 and A5-0189/2003 on multifunctional agriculture and the reform of the CAP?

 
  
 

(EN) The Commission has taken careful note of the question raised by the honourable Member on the classification of rural areas.

There is no single commonly agreed and internationally recognised definition of rural areas, given their great diversity. The Commission has itself no current plans to propose new objective criteria for the definition of a typology of rural areas.

Preparation of any such typology could only be considered for the preparation of rural development policy post 2006. However, even with this longer term perspective, the Commission is not convinced that a common typology defined at Community level is necessary for the implementation of Community rural development policy, nor that a single common typology could take account of the wide diversity of rural situations which will exist in an enlarged Union.

Under Regulation (EC) No 1257/1999 it is currently the responsibility of Member States when preparing their rural development programmes to define what they consider to be rural areas, within their specific national and/or regional contexts.

The Commission considers that the current flexible framework under which Community rural development policy is implemented, which gives a high degree of subsidiarity to Member States, already provides sufficient scope for those Member States or regions who so wish to target the measures within their programmes on the specific needs of rural areas.

Under the 'Less-favoured area measure and areas with environmental restrictions', specified in Chapter V of Council Regulation 1257/1999 there are well-established Community criteria which result:

a)firstly in a distinction between Less favoured rural areas and other rural areas

b)and in a Community typology of handicaps for Less favoured areas:

Natural handicaps (mountain areas; areas north of the 62nd Parallel treated as mountain areas; areas with specific natural handicaps; areas with environmental restrictions resulting from Community law);

Less favoured areas with socio-economic handicaps in danger of abandonment of land-use.

As indicated in the Commission's replies to the European Community Court of Auditors Special Report n° 4/2003 concerning the support for Less favoured areas the evaluation of the results of the current programmes will provide information on the need for a review of the Less favoured areas classification criteria and on the basis of this information the Commission will consider whether the present system should be changed for the period after 2006.

 

Question no 41 by María Esther Herranz García (H-0508/03)
 Subject: Common organisation of the market in fruit and vegetables
 

The recently adopted legislation amending Regulation (EC) 609/2001(1) and Regulation (EC) 412/97(2) governing the creation of producer organisations in the fruit and vegetable sector has not simplified the previous legislative picture: rather, it has complicated it, by offering Member States ample room for manoeuvre when determining the prerequisites for creating such organisations. The new rules state that, should the Member State concerned so wish, producer organisations may market fruit and vegetables not produced by their members. They also authorise participation in their capital by non-producers. These new provisions are liable to create distortions of competition, since they imply a renationalisation of the COM; in addition, they may signal the defeat of the efforts made in the sector to concentrate production.

Can the Commission explain the underlying reasons for these major changes to the COM, and state whether it has made any forecasts of the likely impact of these measures on the market?

 
  
 

(FR) Following the report which the Commission presented to the Council and to Parliament in January 2001 on the working of the common organisation of the markets (COM) in fruit and vegetables and in accordance with the conclusions of the Spanish Presidency following the examination of this report in the Council, there appeared to be an urgent need to simplify the regulations governing fruit and vegetables.

This simplification was necessary in order to simplify the life of the market players and increase legal certainty for them, as well as for the Member States, especially if you think about the impending enlargement and the importance, to the future Member States, of an improved acquis.

The Commission started this process back in December 2002 by reviewing the implementing provisions for this COM. The work was therefore carried out within the framework of the management committee, even though the discussion was extended to the main representatives of the sector and numerous suggestions and comments were received by the Commission from national, regional and European professional organisations, from cooperatives and Producers Organisations (PO).

At the management committee meeting held at the beginning of July 2003, the majority of the delegations supported most of the new provisions for this simplification proposed by the Commission.

The Commission considers that this simplification will help the sector to achieve one of the objectives of the 1996 reform, namely to enable POs to further concentrate the supply of fruit and vegetables. With approximately 30% of production concentrated by the POs on the eve of the reform, the objective set was to achieve 60%. However, this index was still limited to 40% in 2001. According to the Commission, the changes made will make POs better able to respond to the challenges of a highly competitive market and better able to concentrate the supply further, both through transnational POs and associations of POs, which instruments have been strengthened, and through cooperation between different POs, which is widely encouraged by the new rules.

This simplification will also allow the Member States to take better account of the specific practical situations which characterise each of them and with regard to which greater subsidiarity was considered necessary.

 
 

(1) OJ L 90, 30.3.2001, p. 4
(2) OJ L 62, 4.3.1997, p. 16

 

Question no 45 by Bernd Posselt (H-0487/03)
 Subject: Humanitarian situation in the Caucasus
 

How does the Commission assess the latest developments in the humanitarian situation in the Caucasian war zones of Chechnya, Abkhazia and Nagorno-Karabakh?

 
  
 

(EN) The situation in the Northern Caucasus keeps deteriorating, especially with regard to the protection of the 85 000 internally displaced persons (IDPs) in Ingushetia and the provision of humanitarian aid. The Commission is, in particular, extremely concerned at the current situation of IDPs, who are under growing pressure to return to Chechnya. The latest declarations made by Chechen, Ingush and Russian authorities, indicating a few weeks ago that there would be no tent camps left in Ingushetia by the end September, have only added to the diverse forms of pressure put on the IDPs to leave Ingushetia. Aid organisations, among which the European Community Humanitarian Office (ECHO), continue to be forbidden from replacing tents in camps or constructing alternative shelter for IDPs who do not want to return to Chechnya as long as insecurity prevails. The 180 rooms in barracks built by Médecins sans Frontières (MSF) and funded by ECHO still remain empty to date. These are clear factors which point to an increased vulnerability of IDPs present in Ingushetia and to a degradation in the level of protection they are entitled to. In addition, the security situation has deteriorated as well, with armed forces coming from Chechnya extending their mop-up operations to civilians displaced in spontaneous settlements.

In Chechnya proper, the situation keeps deteriorating and it is very likely that violence and insecurity will only increase as elections are approaching. Severe and widespread violations of human rights continue, in particular abductions of civilians at night, tortures and extra-judiciary executions. The humanitarian situation has not improved either so far and people, particularly in Grozny, keep depending largely on external humanitarian aid for their survival.

Unfortunately, access to Chechnya is still extremely difficult, if not impossible, for humanitarian staff and working there entails great risks for aid workers, as the example of Arjan Erkel, the Head of MSF who was abducted 13 months ago, sadly demonstrates. As the honourable Member knows, all demarches to the Russian authorities about Mr Erkel have led nowhere so far, and the Commission counts on your continued support to help it raise the issue whenever possible until his safe liberation is obtained.

As a result of this high insecurity, most aid agencies work through 'remote control' set-ups and rely to a great extent on the extraordinary work of the local staff.

ECHO will continue to support these organisations and maintain the same level of funding for the victims of the crisis in Chechnya: this year's budget is € 26 million. The Commission, through ECHO, remains the largest donor in the region and has already allocated more than € 93 million since the beginning of the second Chechnya conflict. The main sectors ECHO supports are food, non-food, health, water and sanitation, protection, education and shelter. A majority of ECHO's beneficiaries are women and children.

In order to be closer to these beneficiaries and to have more access to Chechnya, ECHO is still trying to open a sub-office in Nazran, Ingushetia. Unfortunately, this proves very difficult since Russian authorities have refused so far to give permission. The Commission hopes that the Russian authorities will eventually respond positively to its request and that it will reach an agreement with them on this issue.

In view of the deterioration of the situation and the many problems ECHO and its partner organisations face in the delivery of aid, the Commission has repeatedly raised the question with the Russian side on numerous occasions over a longer period and the Member of the Commission in charge of Development and Cooperation has personally written twice to the President of Ingushetia to voice his concerns and ask for his support. He hopes this will help facilitating our continued action in favour of the many victims of this terrible crisis.

The situation in Abkhazia has not improved at all from a humanitarian point of view. It has even deteriorated in the last few years, with very little presence of humanitarian organisations. This is why, in view of the extent of the forgotten needs, ECHO has decided in 2002 to renew its support to the most vulnerable populations. ECHO had phased out, like most humanitarian donors, from Abkhazia in 2000, hoping that the situation would improve. Unfortunately, this has not been the case and the absence of a settlement to this frozen conflict has had negative consequences on the economic situation of the region.

ECHO funding for the victims of the Abkhazia conflict, in Abkhazia and in the rest of Western Georgia, amounted to € 1,3 million in 2002 and will amount to € 2,2 million in 2003. This will allow to fund soup kitchens and the distribution of dry food, as well as income and food-generation programmes and food-for-work activities. ECHO will continue to regularly monitor the situation and will have to be engaged as long as humanitarian needs remain.

ECHO is not present in Nagorno-Karabakh but continues to follow the situation and will be ready to intervene, should the situation deteriorate.

 

Question no 46 by Yvonne Sandberg-Fries (H-0489/03)
 Subject: More equitable and effective implementation of payments to NGOs
 

EU bodies such as EAR invite NGOs to participate in particular projects with financial backing from the EU. Some NGOs regard the rules on payment procedures as impractical and obstructive, as EU payments are based inter alia on retrospective part-payments in return for submission of receipts. What this means in practice is that NGOs have to advance large sums, which they often lack the resources to do. Moreover, the final payment of the contractual amount is made only after the work has been completed and the costs incurred and approved by means of a special audit. Consequently it is the NGOs rather than the EU body that have to bear the economic risk. The payment procedure also causes major administrative difficulties for those NGOs whose work depends heavily on the use of volunteers.

Has the Commission assessed, or will it assess, whether these payments are equitable and effective vis-à-vis the parties selected by means of a tender procedure?

 
  
 

(FR) Payment of subsidies to non-governmental organisations (NGOs) from the Union’s general budget comes within the framework of the Financial Regulation applicable to the general budget, especially Articles 79 to 82 and 117 to 119 (and Articles 97, 99, 104-105 and 180 to 183 of the implementing rules). These provisions require the Commission to ensure that proper use is made of Community funds, mainly by limiting the level of prefinancing and demanding audits and financial guarantees. Provision is made for simplified control procedures for NGOs who sign a framework partnership agreement with the Commission on the basis of article 163 of the implementing rules of the Financial Regulation (implementing procedure for humanitarian aid actions).

The flexibility provided for under the implementing rules of the Financial Regulation is used mainly by the Commission to speed up the procedures for making payments to NGOs.

Thus, the procedures for making payments relating to subsidies within the framework of external actions (including by the European Agency for Reconstruction) benefit NGOs. Prefinancing, which may be as much as 80% of the value of the contract (or of the budget for the first year of multiannual contracts) is paid within 45 days of signing of the contract. This prefinancing is much higher than the Commission average. For multiannual contracts, cumulative prefinancing may be as much as 90% of the value of the contract.

In addition, and in order to speed up payments, payments are made simply on presentation and approval of mid-term or final reports (including audit reports where the amount of the payment so warrants). The substantiating documentation is retained by the beneficiary in case the Commission conducts an ex-post audit.

The Commission considers that it cannot make the payment procedures any more flexible without compromising its facility to ensure that actions subsidised are implemented correctly, in accordance with proper financial management.

 

Question no 47 by Richard Howitt (H-0502/03)
 Subject: Transparency of the preparation of mid-term reviews
 

The Commission Communication on 'Participation of Non-State Actors (NSAs) in EC Development Policy' (7/11/02 – COM(2002)0598/final) sought to extend the principle set out in the Cotonou Agreement (in Article 2(4) and (6) inter alia) of NSA participation in each and every stage of the development policy to all regions. Furthermore, the Commission Communication 'Towards a reinforced culture of consultation and dialogue' (COM(2002)0704/final) includes a commitment to open inclusive and non-restrictive dialogue with NSAs in development policy at all levels of policy formulation and implementation. In this context, would the Commission indicate the quality and extent of the dialogue that it plans to hold with NSAs and partner countries in its preparation of the Mid-Term Review? Will the Commission make the criteria on which the reviews are based accessible to NSAs?

 
  
 

(EN) The Mid-Term Reviews of Country and Regional Strategy Papers provide an opportunity to continue and deepen the dialogue with civil society or to begin it in a minority of cases where it has not been established. It as a key moment to make further progress in implementing participatory approaches in the development process.

The Commission will build on the experience gained, drawing on last year’s review of Country Strategy Papers, to make further progress during the 2004 Mid-Term Reviews for African, Caribbean and Pacific (ACP) countries. Detailed guidelines which are currently being prepared will be used. More time will be given for consultation. The Commission will ensure that sufficient information is given to a wide range of non-state actors and that adequate feed-back is provided on the results of the consultation.

The criteria for the Mid-Term Reviews will be made available. The joint annual reports are already made public. They contain all relevant information on the Mid-Term Review process as well as on the use of EDF resources for non-state actors and information on the dialogue and consultation process.

Non-state actors, as well as national Parliamentarians and members of the ACP-EU Joint Parliamentary Assembly will have access to the key elements of the review process. In this way, the Commission is doing its best to ensure transparency and openness.

 

Question no 48 by John Bowis (H-0503/03)
 Subject: Health aspect of Country Strategy Papers and Poverty Reduction Strategy Papers
 

Has the Commission closely monitored and audited the health aspect of Country Strategy Papers and Poverty Reduction Strategy Papers, and by what means - if any – does it plan to ensure more prominence for health schemes and health capacity in its policy papers relating to every developing country that it supports?

 
  
 

(EN) All Country Strategy Papers are subject to an intensive review and quality support process prior to approval. This review includes examination of the choice of focal and non-focal sectors selected and allows the Commission to monitor which countries have included health as a priority.

Concentration of aid in a limited number of focal sectors and national ownership are important principles in country strategy programming. Intervention sectors are chosen on the basis of complementarity with other donors and the comparative advantage of the Commission in the country concerned. As a result, while the Commission consistently promotes health as a development objective, health is not always chosen as a focal sector of Community aid.

Seventeen developing countries have identified health as a focal sector in Country Strategy Papers that were finalised by the end of 2002. In national indicative programmes, the level of allocations for health remains low, but Community funds are increasingly devoted to general budget support. One advantage is that, unlike traditional project aid, budget support provides additional funding for recurrent expenditure, which makes up the major part of the health budget. Budget support may also provide extra money for activities outside the health sector which benefit health, for example improving water supplies and sanitation. In African, Caribbean and Pacific countries 24.4% of 9th European Development Fund programmable resources amounting to over 1.73 billion € are allocated to general budget support. The Commission’s guidelines on health sector programming and monitoring of indicators aim at ensuring the links between poverty reduction budget support strategies and improved health outcomes.

Poverty Reduction Strategy Papers (PRSP) are developed by national authorities in consultation with all key stakeholders. Where a country has a PRSP, the Commission’s policy is that the Country Strategy should be aligned to it. PRSPs provide a single, comprehensive and costed framework for social development priorities and should deliver increased resources for the social sectors. The Commission recently took part in a consultation to review analytical work on health in PRSPs carried out by the World Health Organisation. This demonstrated that the importance of health is generally well reflected in PRSPs, and they include recognised pro-poor health strategies.

 

Question no 49 by Bastiaan Belder (H-0507/03)
 Subject: Provision of information concerning HIV/AIDS in developing countries
 

In the context of development cooperation, the European Commission pays attention to the various aspects of 'safe and responsible sexual behaviour'. What proportion, expressed as percentages, of the measures which the European Commission takes with a view to combating HIV/AIDS are intended to delay the start of an individual's sexual life and reduce the number of sexual partners individuals take? Can the Commission give practical examples of its comprehensive approach highlighting the various aspects of safe and responsible sexual behaviour?

In the context of campaigns to combat HIV/AIDS, what proportion of the social organisations which receive support are faith-based organisations?

Will the European Commission ensure that organisations are not excluded from European funding in connection with measures to combat HIV/AIDS because of their views of sexuality and long-term relationships?

 
  
 

(EN) This reply is a clarification following to earlier questions debated on 10 April and 3 July 2003.

The basis for Community policy in the field of sexual and reproductive health is the International Conference on Population and Development (ICPD) Programme of Action. In relation to HIV/AIDS, sections 8.28-8.35 emphasise providing information and the promotion of safe and responsible sexual behaviour - including voluntary abstinence and the use of condoms. The Commission would also emphasise the importance of providing information about voluntary counselling and testing for HIV (this is mentioned in ICPD+5).

The Commission position is, therefore, that there should be a comprehensive approach to education about sex and sexuality. Young people should be provided with information and encouraged to think and decide for themselves and act responsibly. Such education should also take into account a strong gender perspective. While abstinence supposes 'safe' behaviour with regard to the transmission of HIV it is necessary to be realistic: it is not always the choice or even an option for those sexually active, in particular for women. And for the Commission to rely on preaching or prescribing abstinence as the only option is simply not responsible. Practical information about protecting oneself, in particular concerning the use of condoms, is therefore vital.

It is impossible to say in percentage terms what the relative part of Community efforts are for delaying the age of first sex and reducing the number of sexual partners. Most Community assistance for population-related activities is un-earmarked support for improved health outcomes or general budget support, but even specific support in this area is not conditional on putting more effort into one or other means of preventing HIV/AIDS.

The Commission does not make any specific distinction or category for religious or faith based organisations, although such organisations are and will continue to be welcome to apply for Community support through regular calls for proposals.

To give a concrete example, applicants under the latest call for proposals under the thematic Budget Line B7-6311: 'Programme to combat poverty related diseases in the developing countries, fight against HIV/AIDS' needed to be non-profit making and belong to one of the following categories: non-governmental organisations, public-sector operators distinct from the State, local organisations, research or training institutions. The call's objective was to enhance the relevance and usefulness of programmes to fight HIV/AIDS by improving their impact on young people in general, and young women in particular, and by including the poor and populations at risk.

As a result of the evaluation process, six applicants were selected to receive Community support, including civil organisations such as Danchurch and World Vision Netherlands.

As for all other applicants following this call's guidelines, 'Information, education and communication directed towards changing behaviour will be a priority, bearing in mind that the context (social, health, economic and cultural) must make the desired developments possible'. Safe and responsible sexual behaviour may include delaying the age of first intercourse and reducing the number of sexual partners but it may also include other elements such as the use of condoms or other contraceptive supplies.

Information on the calls for proposals, the selection and eligibility criteria and the actions selected are available on the EuropeAid website.

 

Question no 55 by Patricia McKenna (H-0485/03)
 Subject: Compliance with EU Regulations on substances that deplete the ozone layer
 

The Irish Government is the only EU Member State government not to have reported to the Commission under Regulation (EC) 2037/2000(1) on controlling substances that damage the ozone layer. Can the Commission say what action it is taking to ensure compliance and if the Irish Government could face fines for non-compliance and if so, how much these fines would amount to?

 
  
 

(EN) Ireland shall report steps taken to promote and to establish systems for recovery, recycling, reclamation and destruction of chemicals that destroy the ozone layer, measures introduced to control leakages and set minimum qualification requirements for relevant personnel(2). Ireland has failed to respect these reporting obligations. The Commission is, therefore, using its powers under Article 226 of the EC Treaty to pursue an infringement proceeding(3) against Ireland. On 9 July 2003 the Commission decided to refer Ireland to the Court of Justice. This was the subject of a press release published on 22 July 2003(4).

If the Court of Justice rules that Ireland must report on the above-mentioned issues, Ireland should respect the Court’s ruling. However, if Ireland does not comply with the ruling the Commission can open a new case under Article 228 of the EC Treaty. Ireland could then face a possible daily fine set by the Court of Justice if it still failed to report in accordance with the Regulation(5).

The Commission has published a Memorandum(6) and Communication(7) on its approach to and its method of calculating a proposed daily penalty. However, the level of any daily fine would be a matter for the Court.

 
 

(1) OJ L 244, 29.9.2000, p. 1.
(2) According to Articles 16 and 17 of (EC) Regulation 2037/2000 on Substances that Deplete the Ozone Layer. OJ L 244, 29.9.2000, p.1
(3) Infringement A2002/2142
(4) IP/03/1069
(5) A daily fine being imposed where a Member State fails to comply with a first ruling of the Court of Justice, and the Commission refers the matter back to the Court a second time.
(6) OJ C 242, 21.8.1996
(7) OJ C 63, 28.2.1997

 

Question no 56 by Astrid Thors (H-0498/03)
 Subject: Dioxin strategy for marine environments
 

When the Commission established limit values for the dioxin content of food in late 2001, it indicated that a more comprehensive strategy against dioxins would be drawn up. The strategy drawn up on 24 October 2001 (dioxins, furans and PCBs) stated that further research would be carried out. The strategy to conserve the marine environment also refers to an integrated pilot programme to monitor the effects of dioxins (action 7) and states that the results of monitoring the concentration of dioxins in wild and farmed fish will be produced no later than 2004 (action 15).

What stage has the Commission reached in drawing up the pilot programme to monitor the effects of dioxins? Has the Commission considered supporting targeted fishing in order to reduce dioxin levels in the Baltic Sea? Will the Commission also propose a specific strategy to reduce dioxin levels in fish?

 
  
 

(EN) The pilot programme on 'Integrated dioxin & PCB monitoring in the Baltic Region' has already been launched by the Commission.

It was launched against the backdrop of several other initiatives:

Implementation of the 'Community Strategy for Dioxins, Furans and Polychlorinated Biphenyls' (1) 1

The derogation granted to Sweden and Finland concerning maximum levels for dioxins in fish from the Baltic intended for consumption in that region;

Development of the 'Community Marine Strategy'(2) 2

Implementation of the 'Environment & Health Strategy(3) 3

The pilot programme is being drawn up in three stages:

In October 2002 a meeting of the so-called 'Helcom Monas' 4 was held in Warnemünde. The nine Baltic countries were asked to designate experts on the environment, fish and human health and to give the Commission an overview of the different monitoring activities in the Baltic region.

On 5 May 2003 there was an 'Expert Meeting' in Brussels to discuss a number of issues with dioxin experts from the Baltic Region. These issues included an overview of monitoring activities in the different Baltic Countries, a fish monitoring strategy and the links between environment monitoring and health monitoring.

In September 2003 a 'Technical Working Group on integrated dioxin and PCB monitoring in the Baltic Region' will be established in the framework of the 'Environment and Health Strategy'. The first meeting of this Technical Working Group will be held during the Regional Conference in Poland on 6-7 October 2003. By the end of 2003 it will prepare a Baseline Report, including an overview of existing dioxin and PCB monitoring programmes in the Baltic countries, the problems and shortcomings of existing monitoring systems, and requirements for integrated environment and health dioxin and PCB monitoring. During a second phase, the Working Group will by the end of March 2004 establish options for action and recommendations for the Commission’s 'Environment and Health Action Plan 2004-2010' to be presented as the Community contribution to the Fourth Ministerial Conference on Environment and Health in Budapest in June 2004.

Back in 2001 the Commission proposed a strategy for reducing the presence of dioxins in feed and food, including fish and fishery products as part of the Community Strategy for Dioxins, Furans and Polychlorinated Biphenyls. This strategy, through its integrated approach for reducing the presence of dioxins in feed and food, was to result in a reduction of dioxins levels in fish for human consumption, both wild and farmed.

The Commission is aware of suggestions that catching the fish in which dioxins are concentrated would be a way of cleaning these pollutants out of the Baltic environment. Although the Commission would need much more scientific information before it could fully assess the possible effectiveness of such a measure, the information available at present does not indicate that it would be appropriate as part of a general strategy for reducing dioxin levels in the environment.

 
 

(1) COM(2001)593
(2) COM(2002)539
(3) COM(2003)338

 

Question no 57 by Robert J.E. Evans (H-0515/03)
 Subject: Protection of Cetaceans in EU waters
 

At the recent meeting of the parties to the International Whaling Commission (IWC), the adoption of the Mexican-led Berlin initiative was a great step forward for the welfare of cetaceans worldwide, in particular through the creation of a new conservation committee within the IWC. Given that many Member States are members of the IWC and in light of the EU's aim to protect and conserve the marine environment in an integrated manner, could the Commission provide a summary of all existing legislation to ensure the protection of whales and dolphins in EU waters and explain how these measures will be coordinated with international action?

 
  
 

(EN) At Community level, all cetacean species are protected under the Habitats Directive,(1) the main piece of European legislation aiming at protecting natural habitats and wild fauna. Member States have to take the requisite measures to establish a system of strict protection in order to ensure a favourable status of conservation of cetaceans. Deliberate killing and disturbance of cetaceans as well as the keeping, transporting, sale or exchange of cetaceans or any part of them is banned.

Concerning incidental catches, the Community has, on several occasions, addressed this problem. Community legislation already limits the size of drift nets and has introduced measures to prohibit their use from the start of 2002.(2) The Commission has recently proposed to regulate this issue in a comprehensive manner, extending the ban on driftnets to the Baltic Sea, using acoustic alarms in certain fisheries and instituting a scheme of on-board observers with a view to monitor by-catch.(3)

At international level, the Commission makes every effort to assure that a similar approach is adopted and applied. Thus, when the conservation of cetaceans is discussed within relevant International Conventions and Agreements to which the Community is a party, the position of the Commission is to seek the highest possible level of protection for these species, while respecting the internal procedures established in these international organisms.

Regarding international trade in products obtained from whaling activities, the Community applies the provisions adopted by the Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species of wild fauna and flora, CITES by means of internal regulations(4). Furthermore, the Community goes beyond what CITES requires, in order to ensure consistency with the protection of cetaceans as provided for by the Habitats Directive.

And finally, the Commission welcomes the Berlin Initiative on Strengthening the Conservation Agenda of the International Whaling Commission, as decided in its last meeting, that took place this year. The Commission hopes that this new effort from the IWC will contribute to improved conservation of these species.

 
 

(1) Council Directive 92/43/EEC of 21 May 1992 on the conservation of natural habitats and of wild fauna and flora, O.J. L 206 , 22.7.1992
(2) Council Regulation (EC) No 1239/98 of 8 June 1998 amending Regulation (EC) No 894/97 laying down certain technical measures for the conservation of fishery resources O.J L 171, 17.6.1998.
(3) COM(2003)451, Proposal for a Council Regulation laying down measures concerning incidental catches of cetaceans in fisheries and amending Regulation (EC) No 88/98
(4) Council Regulation (EC) No 338/97 of 9 December 1996 on the protection of species of wild fauna and flora by regulating trade therein, O.J. L 061, 3.3.1997.

 

Question no 58 by Jean Lambert (H-0438/03)
 Subject: Malta's implementation of the Wild Birds Directive
 

In the Accession Treaty signed by Malta on 16 April 2003, Malta committed itself to the full transposition and implementation of the Wild Birds Directive 79/409/EEC(1) by 1 May 2004. Malta revised its laws in January 2003; however these laws are not fully compliant with the Wild Birds Directive. How does the Commission intend to ensure that Malta complies with the Directive from the date of accession? When Malta becomes a member of the Union, the Commission will verify that Council Directive 79/409/EEC on the conservation of wild birds has been correctly transposed, as it does for all Member States. Should Malta not comply with the Directive then the Commission will consider the action to be taken on the basis of the EC Treaty provisions.
