Question no 59 by Othmar Karas (H-0442/03)
 Subject: Different tax systems and impact on the internal market
 

Achieving the main goal of European integration – increasing the wealth of the member countries by removing current obstacles to the free movement of goods and factors – primarily involves dealing with the tax-policy challenges facing the European internal market. The scenario of a Union which already has 15 and will soon have 25 different tax systems raises the question of what consequences these different European tax systems have for the functioning of the internal market. Can these largely negative consequences for business and consumers be quantified? What steps can the Commission take to remedy the situation, particularly bearing in mind that the principle of unanimity applies in tax matters? EU initiatives in the area of taxation must ensure that tax systems contribute to the efficient operation of the internal market, so that the corresponding framework can be created within which to achieve the objective set in Lisbon.

 
  
 

(EN) The Commission is actively looking into the question raised by the honourable Member. For instance, the company tax study prepared in 2001 shows that the need for companies to comply with up to fifteen - and soon twenty-five - different sets of corporate tax rules within the Union gives rise to numerous barriers to cross-border economic activity. These prevent us from achieving the full potential economic benefits of the Internal Market. Without action, the Community and its citizens therefore risk foregoing potential growth, employment and welfare. Progress in this field is also crucial in order to achieve the Lisbon goals.

It is very difficult to determine or quantify the precise effects that the co-existence of 15 and soon 25 differing tax systems have on the functioning of the Internal Market. The Commission is launching in September an extensive survey among businesses in the Union on tax-related compliance costs that it hopes will provide some more insight into the dimension of these problems.

Current economic research does not allow detailed quantification of the size of these welfare losses. They certainly exist and they are substantial but putting a firm figure on their size would be too speculative. This is why there was a need to launch various short-term and longer-term initiatives to address the problems.

The Commission's proposed initiatives will take various forms, ranging from 'soft law' and co-ordination to traditional proposals for Directives. As to the prospect of success of the traditional legislative measures, however, it is true that without changing the unanimity principle for decision taking in Union tax matters, it will be extremely difficult to advance.

 

Question no 60 by Glyn Ford (H-0445/03)
 Subject: Implementation of Article 9 1(a)
 

In the case of the Gatwick-Plymouth-Newquay regional route, which is currently under threat, the UK Government has said that in order to consider imposing a Public Service Obligation, it requires further clarification on how regional routes can be protected under the regulation on current common rules for the allocation of slots at Community airports. In particular, the means of implementation of the provisions in this regulation are not sufficiently clear.

Can the Commission provide clear additional guidelines on how Article 9 1(a) and 9 1(b) of Council Regulation (EEC) 95/93(1) should be implemented by Member States?

 
  
 

(EN) Community law provides for the protection of regional services in two ways.

Firstly, the Community rules on market access provide for Governments to be able to impose the so-called public service obligations. For routes where a public obligation has been imposed and which are domestic, the Community rules on slot allocation provide that member States may reserve slots at fully coordinated airports (article 9 § 1 (b) of the Regulation). For non-domestic routes, where a public service obligation has been imposed, there is no possibility to reserve slots.

Secondly, for routes, which do not benefit from a public service obligation but connect regional airports on the territory of a Member State with a fully coordinated airport, the rules on slot allocation (article 9 § 1 (a) and § 3 of the Regulation) give Member States the right to reserve slots only under certain conditions. Accordingly, slots for such routes could be reserved, only if

the slots concerned are being used on that route at the time of entry into force of this Regulation;

only one air carrier is operating on the route;

no other mode of transport can provide an adequate service;

the reservation of slots shall end when a second air carrier has established a domestic scheduled service on the route with the same number of frequencies as the first air carrier and operated it for at least a season;

Also, as stated in paragraph 3 of article 9 of that Regulation, the Member State concerned has to communicate to the Commission the list of routes fulfilling the conditions set out in article 9 § (a) and (b) and for which slots have been reserved. This shall first be done at the entry into force of the Regulation, that is 22 February 1993, 30 days after its publication in the Official Journal on 22 January 1993, as provided for in article 15 of the Regulation.

To this date the only Member State, which has applied article 9 §1(a) is France. Today, the following routes are operated in application of article 9 § 1 (a) of the Regulation from the airport of Paris-Orly: Annecy, Avignon, Chambéry, Lannion, Limoges and Metz; a total of 6.894 slots have been reserved at Paris-Orly for these routes.

 
 

(1) OJ L 14, 22.1.1993, p. 1

 

Question no 61 by Esko Olavi Seppänen (H-0448/03)
 Subject: Taxation of emission allowances
 

A jointly approved directive (report A5-0207/2003) recommends trading in emission allowances which would be granted to businesses to permit the emission of carbon dioxide into the atmosphere. The Commission proposal did not deal adequately with the issue of taxation of emission allowances. By means of what provisions will the Commission ensure that income tax, wealth tax, capital gains tax and value added tax are levied on the same basis in all Member States, whether or not emission allowances are traded?

 
  
 

(FR) The Commission points out that the sale or retention of emission rights, from a fiscal point of view, will not be determined – at least as regards tax on income, capital and capital gains (derived from the sale of the said rights) – in the same way in the different Member States, given that these taxes are not harmonised at European level. As regards indirect taxation, the sale of emission rights will be construed as a provision of services as defined in the Sixth VAT Directive

(1) and will be subject to VAT in all the Member States.

As the Commission has already observed during the amendments to the proposal tabled by Parliament at first reading, the directive cannot create fiscal obligations for the Member States which go beyond its scope. On questions of taxation, the Member States continue to have competence although, as the Court of Justice has stressed on several occasions, the exercise of this competence cannot release them from the obligation to comply with Community law.

The Commission, however, is aware of the material issues raised and of the fact that the lack of coordination between the Member States' tax systems still carries risks of distortion on the internal market (refer, for example, to the answer to written question P-0028/02(2)).

In - partially - accepting another amendment to the draft directive proposed by Parliament, the Commission felt that all the aspects relating to taxation (including capital gains and losses on emission rights bought and sold) were sufficiently interesting to warrant being highlighted in the report on the application of the directive which the Member States are required to submit to the Commission each year. On the basis of these national reports, the Commission will publish a report which may, if necessary, highlight tax problems linked to the application of the directive and the proper working of the internal market.

 
 

(1) Council Directive 77/388/EEC of 17 May 1977 on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States relating to turnover taxes – Common system of value added tax: uniform basis of assessment
(2) OJ L

 

Question no 62 by Lennart Sacrédeus (H-0451/03)
 Subject: The EMU Stability Pact and active measures to combat the recession
 

On 2 July, speaking on behalf of the Italian Presidency, Prime Minister Silvio Berlusconi admitted in the European Parliament that the members of the Euro zone no longer had any financial and monetary means of combating recessions and that instead there was a need for ‘European measures’, an active European financial policy and measures by the European Investment Bank. The Council’s new President also called for a relaxation of the EMU Stability and Growth Pact for the euro.

What is the Commission’s view of the proposal made in June/July by France’s Defence Minister, Michèle Alliot-Marie, partly with the support of her German counterpart Peter Struck, that purchases of armaments should be regarded not as expenditure but as investment, so that they would have no bearing on the balanced-budget requirement, and the suggestion by Italy’s Minister for European Affairs, Rocco Buttiglione , that research funding should be treated in the same way?

 
  
 

(EN) The Treaty obliges countries to avoid excessive deficit positions (defined as general government deficit below a reference value of 3 per cent of gross domestic product), and the Stability and Growth Pact (SGP) requires countries to achieve budget positions ‘close to balance or in surplus’. The concept of ‘general government’ is defined in the Council Regulation N° 2223/96, and the corresponding statistical data are the ones in ESA95. All the expenditures included within the ‘general government’ definition do have a bearing on the ‘close to balance or in surplus’ requirements and on the excessive deficit. Both defence and research expenditure are part of the expenditure of ‘general government’.

The Commission has always, within the respect of the SGP framework, favoured the improvement of the quality of the public expenditure. In its Communication on ‘Strengthening the co-ordination of budgetary policies’ the Commission proposed to take into account major structural reforms (including tax and investment) in line with the Lisbon Strategy when assessing compliance with the ‘close to balance or in surplus’ requirement.

On the other hand, the Commission does not support any proposals to exclude specific spending items from the calculation of government expenditure or deficits.

 

Question no 63 by Bill Newton Dunn (H-0454/03)
 Subject: Special Representative for Tibet
 

In the current Commission Budget - in line B8-012 - there are both commitments and payments which are 'also intended to finance the activities of an EU Special Envoy for Tibet'.

What progress has the Commission made with appointing a Special Envoy for Tibet in accordance with the Budgetary Authority's wishes and to match the action already taken in Washington DC? If it has no progress to report - why not? And does the Commission have the political will to make such an appointment?

 
  
 

(FR) The situation in Tibet, including the maintenance and preservation of that country's cultural, linguistic and religious identity, is one of the European Union's priorities in the framework of its overall policy vis-à-vis China.

The communication channels that already exist between Peking and the Union have enabled the latter to bring its concerns in this regard to the Chinese authorities' attention on a very regular basis, be it at sessions of the bilateral Human Rights dialogue or at the very highest political level, during summit meetings.

In this connection, the Union has repeatedly called for direct dialogue to be established between the Dalai Lama and the Chinese authorities, which it considers to be the only realistic way of one day finding a lasting solution to the question of Tibet. It welcomes the recent re-establishing of contacts during the second visit of the Dalai Lama's envoys to China and would like to see these intensified. It is resolved to continue to support any initiative which might serve to solve the question of Tibet and is acceptable to all the parties concerned within the framework of genuine autonomy for that region.

In the light of these developments, it wonders whether the appointment of an EU Special Envoy for Tibet would in any way enhance the current dialogue mechanism and whether, moreover, it would not be liable to trigger a reaction from China that would compromise the process of reconciliation initiated between the Tibetan representatives and the Chinese authorities and divest the Union of any mediating capacity.

It is also doubtful as to what scope an EU Special Envoy would have for effective action in Tibet. The experience of the US Special Envoy would not appear to be altogether conclusive in this respect.

 

Question no 64 by Niall Andrews (H-0459/03)
 Subject: New initiatives for young people in the enlarged European Union
 

Given the future enlargement of the European Union in 2004 and the need to build a Europe of and for young people, will the Commission outline the new, innovative ways in which it considers that this can be achieved?

 
  
 

(FR) The Commission has long been of the opinion that the European Union must be developed not only for young people but also with the assistance of young people. That is why it has run action programmes such as Socrates, Leonardo and Youth which are aimed at young people and which enable them to take part in activities at European level in the fields of education, training and youth work. The programmes are already open to young people from the applicant countries in accordance with the terms and conditions laid down in European agreements, in the additional protocols thereto and in the decisions taken by the respective Association Councils. From 2004 the programmes will also be open to young people from Turkey.

When in November 2001 the Commission published its White Paper entitled 'A new impetus for European youth', it did so after having conducted a huge consultation exercise involving young people and youth organisations in both the Member States and the applicant countries. In its White Paper the Commission proposed establishing a new youth-cooperation framework based on an open coordination method in which 'applicant countries are associated as far as possible'. This proposal was adopted by the Council in its 27 June 2002 resolution on a cooperation framework for young people (OJ C 168, 13.7.2002).

In that resolution the Commission was instructed to send out questionnaires to the Member States and, on that basis, to propose common objectives for the four priorities identified in the White Paper and adopted by the Council in the resolution. The initial questionnaires sent out were concerned with the topics of participation and information provision. In its analysis of the replies received the Commission took into account the situation and the expectations of the applicant countries to which the questionnaires had also been sent out. The common objectives are currently on the Council table. In addition, many applicant countries consulted their young people and youth organisations before sending in their replies to the Commission. This consultation exercise (which follows on from the one carried out in connection with the White Paper) is an innovative tool preparing the way for enlargement, since it has enabled young people from the applicant countries themselves to become involved in the process of devising a youth policy. In order to increase young people's involvement in the political process the Commission is planning to issue in 2004 a call for proposals for pilot schemes which will encourage active citizenship on the part of young people. That call will be open to youth organisations in the new Member States.

The existing Youth programme will terminate at the end of 2006, for which reason the Commission has conducted a huge consultation exercise with a view to drawing up the next programme covering the 2007-2012 period. It intends to submit at the beginning of 2004 a proposal relating to young people and also to education and vocational training. The proposal will take full account of the needs of young people in an enlarged Europe.

Furthermore, the inclusion of young people within the employment market and within society as a whole is a major consideration in EU employment strategy and in EU social-inclusion strategy. The applicant countries are working in close cooperation with the Commission in order to prepare themselves for the implementation of those strategies.

 

Question no 65 by Brian Crowley (H-0461/03)
 Subject: Promoting EU/USA exchanges for young people
 

The Thessaloniki Presidency conclusions confirm that the development of transatlantic relations on an equal footing remains of fundamental importance to the EU and that the EU is determined to develop transatlantic dialogue at all levels.

As part of this process, will the Commission state how it can promote cultural, economic and educational exchanges for the benefit of young people in the enlarged EU and the United States of America in the coming years?

 
  
 

(EN) The main vehicle for multilateral educational exchanges between the Union and the United States (which have also a cultural and economic dimension) is the cooperation programme in the field of higher education and vocational training established by the agreement between the Community and the United States concluded in 1995. This programme was renewed for a further five-year period (2001-2005) concluded in 2001(1).

The programme aims primarily at promoting understanding between the peoples of the Community and the United States and improving the quality of their human resource development.

To achieve these objectives the Community and the United States jointly support - within the constraints of a very limited budget - innovative, multilateral, student-centred projects with the potential to stimulate substantive and long-lasting structural transatlantic co-operation in higher education and vocational education and training. It may also support international education projects that give rise to new forms of cooperation between the United States and the Community.

Both the Community and the United States are committed to continuing and reinforcing cooperation in education and training within the present programme and beyond. The participation of the new Member States in the cooperation programmes with the United States as from 1 May 2004 depends on the relevant budget for 2004, which has not been fixed.

 
 

(1) see Council Decision 2001/196/EC, published in the Official Journal of the European Community on 26 February 2001.

 

Question no 66 by James (Jim) Fitzsimons (H-0467/03)
 Subject: 'Neighbourhood Programmes'
 

The Commission has indicated that it intends to improve the EU’s interventions at its external borders in order to boost cooperation with Russia, the Western Balkans and countries in the Mediterranean. Existing instruments such as PHARE-CB and MEDA will be better coordinated through the creation of ‘Neighbourhood Programmes’.

Will the Commission state if this means that countries such as the Lebanon and Syria will benefit from this new proposal and, if so, in what ways?

 
  
 

(EN) The Commission has adopted the Communication on 'Wider Europe' as a framework for developing relations with the countries that will become the Union’s new Eastern and Southern neighbours after enlargement.. In this 'Wider Europe' framework, the Union will work with partners to develop a zone of prosperity and a friendly neighbourhood, with whom the Union enjoys close, peaceful and co-operative relations.

As part of this policy, the Commission will introduce new neighbourhood programmes using the established programmes for cooperation with neighbouring countries - primarily TACIS for the countries of the Community of Independent States (CIS), CARDS for Western Balkans and MEDA for the countries on the Eastern and Southern shores of the Mediterranean, combined with INTERREG for the Union side of the programmes. A major theme of the Neighbourhood programmes will be to promote regional and transnational cooperation as well as cross-border cooperation. This will pave the way for introducing a New Neighbourhood Instrument after 2006.

Both Lebanon and Syria will be eligible to benefit from these programmes , once they fulfil the criteria on concluding Association Agreements with the Community and its Member States.

 

Question no 67 by José Manuel García-Margallo y Marfil (H-0468/03)
 Subject: Imports of ceramic products from China
 

China has been a member of the WTO since 11 December 2001. Despite this, there are still a number of discriminatory measures (price control policies, totally arbitrary health and certification requirements, obstacles to investment and serious deficiencies in the protection of trademarks) which make a proper commercial relationship between the EU and China on an equal footing difficult. In 2001 China was the world’s leading producer of ceramic floor-tiles, with a 36.4% share of world production and exports of ceramic floor tiles from China grew by 122%.

In view of this and the provisions of Council Regulation (EC) 427/2003(1), is the Commission aware of the serious harm the situation is causing to the Community ceramic industry? Will it take steps to ensure that the problem can be solved as soon as possible, without waiting for the European market to be invaded by products from China?

 
  
 

(EN) The Commission is in close contact with the Community industry of ceramic tiles, in particular concerning the development of imports of ceramic tiles at low prices from the People's Republic of China.

In relation to the steps that the Commission could take in order to provide relief against injurious and/or unfair imports from China, such steps can only be taken within the framework of the existing trade defence instruments, i.e. the anti-dumping, the anti-subsidy and the safeguard regulations (erga omnes safeguard regulation or the product specific safeguard mechanism against Chinese imports provided in Council Regulation (EC) No 427/2003 which implements China's Protocol of Accession to the World Trade Organisation).

The application of measures under any of the above-mentioned instruments is subject to international and Community rules. Thus, the initiation of any investigation must be based on prima facie evidence that imports of products of Chinese origin are causing material or serious injury to a Community industry. This analysis is based on the negative development of a certain number of factors, such as market shares, financial performance and employment. The anti-dumping and anti-subsidy instruments furthermore require prima facie evidence of dumping (price discrimination or selling under costs of production) or of the provision of State aid to the exporters. If such prima facie evidence is presented to the Commission, the Commission is prepared to initiate an investigation in order to determine whether the conditions for the imposition of measures are met.

So far, the Commission has not received any official request for the initiation of an investigation regarding imports of ceramic tiles from China under any of the above-mentioned trade defence instruments. Needless to say, the Commission is always ready to provide assistance to the Community ceramic industry and Member States for the examination of the merits of a potential complaint under existing trade defence instruments, in particular when small and medium sized are involved.

 
 

(1) OJ L 65, 8.3.2003, p.1.

 

Question no 68 by Maurizio Turco (H-0472/03)
 Subject: Transposition in Italy of the directive against discrimination at work
 

The Italian Council of Ministers has approved the legislative decree implementing Directive 2000/78/EC(1) against discrimination at work based on religion or belief, disability, age or sexual orientation. The decree gives rise to doubts about the correct and full application of the anti-discrimination directive, in particular as regards sexual orientation and religion. In this connection does the Commission not consider that the directive is being transposed excessively vaguely, without limiting, as the Directive itself does, possible exceptions to the ban on discrimination, in particular the wording of Article 3(3) of the decree, which allows discrimination contrary to the directive, and hence that the decree is contrary to the spirit and letter of the directive, in particular as regards religion and sexual orientation? If the directive were to be implemented in Italy in accordance with these provisions, would the Commission be obliged to take Italy to the Court of Justice?

 
  
 

(EN) The Commission is aware that Italy has approved the legislative decree implementing Directive 2000/78/EC establishing a general framework for equal treatment in employment and occupation.(2)

The Commission will examine the legislative decree to ensure that it is a correct and complete transposition of the requirements of the Directive. If according to this analysis the legislative decree is not in conformity with the provisions of the Directive, the Commission will take the appropriate measures to ensure that Italy complies with its obligations arising from the Directive. In particular, once the deadline for the transposition of the Directive has passed on 2 December 2003, the Commission can make use of its powers under Article 226 of the Treaty and initiate infringement proceedings should it consider this necessary.

 
 

(1) OJ L 303, 2.12.2000, p. 16.
(2) The legislative decree was adopted on 9 July 2003. It will be published soon in the Official Compilation of legislative acts of the Italian Republic (Raccolta ufficiala degli atti normativi della Repubblica Italiana).

 

Question no 69 by Marialiese Flemming (H-0473/03)
 Subject: Trans-European Transport Networks/Danule-Oder-Elbe Canal
 

With regard to the proposal for a revision of the Trans-European Transport Network (TEN-T) guidelines expected later this year, how does the Commission intend to ensure that priority projects in both the Member States and Accession Countries fully comply with the requirements of EC environmental directives, particularly the Habitats Directive, the Wild Birds Directive and the Water Framework Directive?

Concerns have been expressed about the impact of the Danube-Oder-Elbe canal on sites and species requiring protection under EC legislation in both the Member States and Accession Countries. Will EU funds be allocated to the development of this canal? What action is the Commission taking to ensure that the project is compatible with the provisions of the Birds, Habitats and Water Framework Directives?

 
  
 

(EN) The TEN-T Guidelines (1996) make reference to current environmental regulations in Art 8.

At the level of individual projects, according to the Directive on the assessment of the effects of certain public and private projects on the environment(1), Member States must carry out an environmental assessment.

At the level of programmes, they have to implement the Directive on the assessment of the effects of certain plans and programmes on the environment(2) by July 2004. Article 6 of the Habitats Directive requires Member States also to assess plans (and projects) in relation to their impact on Natura 2000 sites. The subject of these regulations are plans and programmes of the Member States including those in the transport sector: for these a strategic environmental assessment should be carried out to protect the environment before the project is planned. The strategic environmental assessment is a contribution to promote sustainable development. This relates to the Water Framework Directive(3) also, which has to be transmitted into national law at the end of 2003.

The Commission proposes to assist the Member States in working out appropriate methods for strategic evaluation and corridor analysis.

Furthermore, the Commission would recall that, in line with the principles of good governance, it is undertaking a detailed impact study of the recommendations of the High Level Group which, under the chairmanship of Mr Van Miert has identified a number of priority TEN-T projects. This study, which will consider the impact on all aspects relating to sustainable development, will be taken into account in the forthcoming adoption of the Commission proposal on revising the TEN-T guidelines in an enlarged Europe.

From a legal point of view, Community legislation will only apply in the new Member States from accession onwards. However, the Commission takes the view that they should apply and implement the provisions of the environmental acquis during the pre-accession period. In cases where Community pre-accession funding of large infrastructure developments is involved, 'similar standards' as set by Community environmental legislation need to be applied before any Commission funding is given to the accession countries.

TEN-T funds have not been allocated to the development of the Danube-Oder-Elbe canal and the Regional Fund is not involved in this project, which is not part of the existing TEN-T guidelines and has not been identified by the High Level Group as a priority project.

 
 

(1) Council Directive 85/337/EEC of 27 June 1985 (Official Journal L 175 of 5.7.1985) amended by Council Directive 97/11/EC of 3 March 1997
(2) Directive 2001/42/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 27 June 2001 (Official Journal L 197 of 21.7.2001)
(3) Directive 2000/60/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 23 October 2000 (Official Journal L 327 of 22.12.2000)

 

Question no 70 by Karin Riis-Jørgensen (H-0476/03)
 Subject: Proposal for a directive of the European Parliament and the Council on intermodal loading units
 

The proposal for a directive of the European Parliament and the Council (COM(2003)0155/final) on intermodal loading units has been criticised for disregarding the fact that, in a global market, it is not possible to divide off a particular market such as the European market, in which the proposed loading unit would be used, when that unit is not used in other parts of the world.

How does the Commission reconcile the introduction of a regional standard (in this case a European standard for containers) with its own statements pointing out that the existence of many different standards for containers poses a problem, and on what calculations does the Commission base its conclusion that the costs of adapting existing container ships would be no more than marginal?

What arguments can the Commission put forward to justify its proposal to create a European control system for all containers, when this function has been performed satisfactorily by the national authorities under the current CSC Convention?

 
  
 

(FR) A distinction needs to be made between three types of loading units:

Containers which comply with ISO standards will continue to move freely in Europe. These containers account for over 91% of the capacity of the world fleet of loading units. However, it should be emphasised that they are not optimised for intermodal transport and logistics in Europe, which means they are ill-suited to and rarely used for intra-European transport.

Containers which do not comply with ISO standards,(1) particularly those over 40 feet long, account for nearly 4% of the said capacity. These containers may continue to be used for sea, river or rail transport, but they do not comply with the maximum dimensions authorised for roads in Europe. They cannot, therefore, be transported by road.

Swap bodies used in Europe account for approximately 2.7% of the capacity of the world fleet of loading units. They are specific to the continent, which explains why they are frequently seen. Unlike the two preceding categories, the majority of swap bodies do not come under the Convention for Safe Containers. They are standardised at European level by the CEN(2), but there are several models which are not standardised. The variety of models causes handling problems during transhipment.

The Commission proposal has three objectives:

To extend controls carried out by the national authorities within the framework of the International Convention on Safe Containers to swap bodies which do not currently come within its scope.

To simplify handling by ensuring that the equipment used to handle containers can be used to handle swap bodies. This is the objective of the project to standardise handling and stowing interfaces for the new intermodal loading units (ILU). The idea is to come into line with the configurations of these interfaces for ISO containers.

The main objective of the proposal for a directive is to launch a European intermodal loading unit (EILU). Use of the EILU will not be compulsory. It will have advantages which will help it to win market shares, because it will be a compromise between the solidity of a container and the capacity of a swap body. In addition, it will be suitable for travel both by sea and waterways and by rail and road. The tasks relating to the finalisation of handling arrangements for the new ILUs and the definition of the EILU will be entrusted to European standardisation organisations (such as the CEN), which will liaise with the ISO. If the CEN can find a technical solution for a unit which respects the guide spacing in cellular container ships, while allowing two front palettes to be loaded, the problem will be resolved. At all events, it is conceivable that EILUs will be able to travel on deck, with the hold reserved for containers. It is a question of opening up for short sea shipping a market in which it currently has no foothold: the swap body market.

 
 

(1) International Organization for Standardization
(2) European Committee for Standardization

 

Question no 71 by Yasmine Boudjenah (H-0477/03)
 Subject: Negotiation of ACP-EU Economic Partnership Agreements
 

One year after the opening of negotiations on ACP-EU Economic Partnership Agreements, the ACP countries confirmed their willingness to comply with the scheduled timetable, while reaffirming, however, that the first phase of the negotiations should lead to a formal undertaking by the two parties on the principles and objectives of the EPAs and the issues of common interest to all the ACP countries. With the opening of the second, ‘regional negotiation’, phase scheduled for September 2003, can the Commission confirm that the first phase has indeed proved sufficient ‘to enable a clear definition of the future EPAs’ objectives to emerge’, as called for by Parliament on 26 September 2002 in the resolution I had the honour of presenting as rapporteur?

The Commission announced that it would carry out impact studies of these agreements with a view to establishing free-trade areas. Has it carried out such studies and/or helped the ACP countries to carry out their own impact assessments? The Commission undertook to keep Parliament regularly informed on the progress of the negotiations, possibly in the form of annual reports. What action has been taken on this?

 
  
 

(EN) In the all-African, Caribbean and Pacific (ACP) phase, progress in substance has been slow. Much time was taken, especially at the beginning, for agreeing on organisational matters. However, in the last months, the ACP and the Commission have covered some ground. For example, since mid-June 2003 six meetings in the so-called 'dedicated format' have taken place, covering areas such as legal issues, the development dimension, services, agriculture, market access and trade related areas.

In terms of outcome of the discussions, the all-ACP – Commission talks have generated an overall understanding on important principles (for example on flexibility and regional integration). In many meetings, the development dimension of European Partnership Agreements (EPA) was at the centre of discussions, which has led to increasingly common views on this issue. For example, it was agreed that while EPAs would directly contribute to the development of ACP countries, they would need to be accompanied by appropriate development assistance so as to allow to maximise the benefits. Divergences remain notably on the question of additional resources.

The ACP side and the Commission agreed at the last meeting of ACP Trade Ministers on 31 July 2003 in Brussels, that the dialogue on the all-ACP level will continue when regional negotiations start from October onwards.

The Commission is working with the ACP on a joint report on the first phase for the next joint meeting on EPAs on ministerial level, which is envisaged for 1 October 2003 in Brussels. This event will be followed by the start of negotiations on a regional level with West-Africa and Central-Africa.

Studies and information

In terms of ex-ante assessments of the negotiations, work has advanced in the last months. The support programme of € 20 million for the preparations of EPA negotiations has proven to be a useful source for national and regional impact assessments by the ACP. As a first step, regional impact assessments were carried out for more than € 1,1 million. More recently, studies for 17 ACP countries have been kick started for a total of € 2,4 million. Furthermore, institutional capacity programmes for four countries and three larger regional support programmes for a total of € 600.000 and € 2,5 million, respectively, have been approved. Additionally to the € 20 million programme, more funds will be soon available for trade-related analysis: an all-ACP programme for trade capacity of € 50 million was approved by the European Development Fund (EDF) committee in July 2003 and should be operational by beginning/mid 2004. Regional and national programmes under the 9th EDF offer considerable resources for negotiation-related analysis and capacity building. In the regional programmes alone, about € 350 million are foreseen for regional integration and trade, including EPA negotiations.

The first phase of the Sustainability Impact Assessment (SIA) financed by the Commission will be summarised in a final report, expected for October. Before, the preliminary results will be discussed with civil society in two workshops in W-Africa and the Caribbean, in order to maximise stakeholder inputs and participation. The latest documents of the SIA are accessible on the webpage of the Consultant (.

On the question of transparency and, in particular, communication with the Parliament, both, the Commissioner in charge of Development and Cooperation, and the one in charge of Trade, have been active during the last year to answer questions and to report on the status of negotiations. Status of negotiations was also discussed in a number of meetings with civil society in Brussels, organised by the Commission. Documents regarding the negotiations have been posted on the Directorate General Trade website. Finally, the joint ACP-Commission report on the first phase will be available as soon as agreed, probably beginning of October.

 

Question no 72 by Myrsini Zorba (H-0479/03)
 Subject: Hydrogen
 

The hydrogen economy was one of the items on the agenda of discussions at the EU-US summit in June. According to the joint statement, cooperation between the two parties forms 'part of our broadening cooperation on energy ' and concerns the 'technical, legal, and commercial basis'.

Will the Commission provide further information and data concerning cooperation, especially in the field of research, and the fostering of public-private collaboration? Will it also provide the timetable and action plan relating to 'International Partnership for the Hydrogen Economy'?

 
  
 

(FR) The importance of cooperation on technological research and development in the hydrogen sector has been recognised - at the highest level - in a declaration annexed to the conclusions of the last transatlantic summit (25 June 2003).

This cooperation will be implemented within the framework of existing cooperation agreements: the scientific and technical (S&T) cooperation agreement of 5 December 1997, the agreement implementing S&T cooperation in the field of non-nuclear energy signed on 14 May 2001 and the rider on fuel cells signed on 16 June 2003.

The S&T cooperation mechanisms described in these agreements are implemented in accordance with the participation rules defined in the sixth framework programme and apply to public and private actors in the various eligible countries, including the United States.

The potential participation of the European Commission in the International Partnership for the Hydrogen Economy (IPHE) initiative proposed by the American Department of Energy (DoE) will likewise come within this context. This participation will be consistent with the Commission's political undertakings in this and related fields (energy, environment, trade aspects etc.) and will not under any circumstances replace measures under way at Community level (European Hydrogen and Fuel Cell Technology Platform, in particular) and at international level (work of the International Energy Agency, for example).

The proposal by the IPHE is being examined by the various Commission services involved as we speak. This proposal - which is still provisional and is to be amended by the future members of the IPHE - does not include a precise action plan or timetable, with the exception of the planned date for launching the initiative in Washington on 19 November 2003.

 

Question no 73 by Christos Folias (H-0481/03)
 Subject: Electronic games
 

In his answer to my Written Question P-3281/02 the Commissioner responsible states that, in accordance with Article 226 of the EC Treaty, a letter of formal notice has been sent to the Greek authorities. He goes on to state that the Commission’s services are ready to examine elements of the law falling under Article 43 and Article 49 of the EC Treaty and Directive 98/34/EC(1).

Given that seven months have elapsed since then, will the Commission say how the Greek authorities have responded to the letter of formal notice? What emerged from the examination of the elements of the law falling under Article 43 and Article 49 of the EC Treaty and Directive 98/34/EC? What does it intend to do on the basis of the new circumstances?

 
  
 

(EN) The Commission has been closely following the Greek ban, as from 1 August 1st 2002, on the installation and operation of all electric, electronic and electromechanical games, amusement-technical games and all games used by means of computers included, in all public or private places except for casinos, introduced by Greek law 3037/2002.

Following the enactment of the law and having received several complaints, the Commission sent, on October 18th 2002, a letter of formal notice to the Greek authorities whereby it was noted that the law 3037/2002 introduces measures that may, contrary to article 28 of the EC Treaty, have an effect equivalent to quantitative restrictions to imports of games and related goods from other Member States and that those measures seem to be unjustified and disproportionate.

The Greek authorities replied claiming that the measures introduced by law 3037/2002 are justified on the grounds of public morality, public policy and public security provided for by article 30 of the EC Treaty.

In the meanwhile the Commission has however come to the conclusion that certain provisions of the Greek law appear to be also contrary to the freedom to provide services, the freedom of establishment as well as to Directive 98/34/EC which obliges Member States to notify draft technical regulations and rules on Information Society Services. It therefore sent on July 11th an additional letter of formal notice to the Greek authorities and issued a press-release on this subject on July 22nd (2). A reply by the Greek authorities is due by mid-September.

Question no 74 by Proinsias De Rossa (H-0482/03)
 Subject: Future of cancer networks
 

Will the Commission please confirm that the internal selection committee for the new public health action plan of the European Commission did not select any of the applications of the existing cancer networks? Will the Commission please indicate the likely effect of this decision on the EU policy of prevention of cancer and its unique and highly developed instruments for cancer prevention? Will the Commission further indicate how it now proposes to develop further the European guidelines on breast cancer, to the benefit of all European women?

 
  
 

(EN) While a final decision will be made in the autumn, it seems unlikely that the projects referred to by the honourable Member can be financed. The programme budget was heavily over-subscribed. Consequently, the great majority of applications, including many of very high quality and importance, simply cannot be funded.

The budget for the health determinants part of the new Public Health Action programme is roughly the same as that for the old 'Europe Against Cancer' programme (about €15 million). However, with the same amount, a much vaster range of activities needs to be financed. The cancer projects referred to by the honourable Member would use a budgetary bracket amounting to nearly the entire budget available for a vast field like health determinants. Bearing in mind that the 'Europe Against Cancer' programme has come to an end and that funding possibilities under the new Public Health Action programme for activities covered by the former are much more limited, these networks will need to pursue alternative sources of funding.

Community support for cancer prevention as such does, however, not come to an end; indeed, a number of cancer-related projects are likely to be funded under the present round of financing. These include projects on the establishment of cancer indicators, smoking prevention and smoking cessation, alcohol prevention and on nutrition. These projects should produce sustainable results that will also contribute to cancer prevention, both directly and indirectly.

As far as developing European guidelines for breast cancer screening is concerned, highly developed instruments are available. Reference is made here in particular to the 'European Guidelines for Quality Assurance in Mammography Screening', the 4th edition of which will be published in 2004. The Commission will analyse carefully the need for any future updating of this document and the possibility of further support that could be given by the Community.

Finally, in response to new developments, such as the Council Recommendation on Cancer Screening, which will be adopted by the Italian Presidency, the 2004 Work Plan of the Public Health Action programme may well include a specific priority area on cancer prevention.

 

Question no 75 by Nuala Ahern (H-0488/03)
 Subject: OSPAR
 

The Final declaration of the Ministerial Meeting of the 'OSPAR' Commission, made in Bremen on 25 June 2003, said on radioactive substances at point (6) that the objective is 'to prevent pollution of the maritime area from ionising radiation through progressive and substantial reductions of discharges, emissions and losses of radioactive substances, with the ultimate aim of concentrations in the environment near background values for naturally occurring radioactive substances and close to zero for artificial radioactive substances. In achieving this objective, the following issues should, inter alia, be taken into account: (a) legitimate uses of the sea; (b) technical feasibility; and (c) radiological impacts on man and biota' and at point (7), the timeframe is that 'by the year 2020 the [OSPAR] Commission will ensure that discharges, emissions and losses of radioactive substances are reduced to levels where the additional concentrations in the marine environment above historic levels, resulting from such discharges, emissions and losses, are close to zero.'

What contribution are the Commission, the JRC and EURATOM respectively making to help meet the aim of radically reducing radioactive releases to the marine environment from nuclear installations such as Sellafield in the United Kingdom?

 
  
 

(EN) The Commission participated fully in the recent OSPAR(1) Ministerial meeting in Bremen referred to by the honourable Member.

As recalled in the ministerial statement at Bremen, the strategy concerning radioactive substances, which was adopted by OSPAR at Sintra in 1998, has the specific objective that by the year 2020 discharges, emissions and losses of radioactive substances are reduced to levels where the additional concentrations in the marine environment above historic levels, resulting from such discharges, emissions and losses, are close to zero. The OSPAR commission is assisted in the implementation of this strategy by its Radioactive Substances Committee (RSC).

As the Community is a Contracting Party to the OSPAR Convention, it is represented at OSPAR meetings by the Commission and, as such, is firmly committed to the attainment of the objectives of the OSPAR strategy with regard to radioactive substances. The Commission, including the Joint Research Center, is an active participant in the RSC and its various working groups and contributes significantly to the understanding and awareness of the discharge of radioactive substances from all sources into the maritime area..

As part of this contribution, the Commission recently completed a study known as MARINA II, which examined all radioactive discharges and their effects on the OSPAR maritime area. MARINA II, which has been presented to OSPAR, has laid the scientific foundation for building a consensus on the definition of a baseline for radioactive substances against which progress on the implementation of the OSPAR strategy can be measured. Importantly, such consensus on the baseline was also reached at the Bremen ministerial meeting in June.

Furthermore, the Commission has recently outlined, in a communication to the Council and the Parliament, a framework for an Union strategy for the protection and conservation of the marine environment. Action 10 of that communication provides for a review of the OSPAR process and for consideration of the need for additional Community measures. Taking account of the outcome of the Bremen Conference, the Commission will consider what further actions are required in this context.

 
 

(1) Oslo-Paris Commission

 

Question no 76 by Olivier Dupuis (H-0491/03)
 Subject: Lao Hmong
 

According to journalists arrested in Laos while preparing a report on the Hmong in the province of Xieng Khouang, 5 or 6 groups of Lao Hmong are living there in desperate conditions. These groups, who are forced to live on a diet of roots, ‘have been subjected to a policy of brutal repression by the Vientiane authorities for decades. With no other weapons but old AK-47s and patched up M16s, and with no ammunition, ‘they aspire to peace and a normal life’. According to the journalists, no solution will be found without mediation by ‘a third party’, since the Lao Hmong have no confidence in the Laotian authorities.

Is the Commission aware of the tragic situation of the Hmong people in Laos? What initiatives does it intend to take to end the fierce repression they have suffered for almost 40 years? Would the Commission be willing to act as a mediator between the Hmong groups and the Vientiane government with a view to achieving a rapid and fair solution to the problem, and is it prepared to launch an exceptional operation to offer the 2 000 or so Hmong refuge on EU territory?

 
  
 

(EN) The Commission is aware that ethnic minority groups in Laos often face difficulties. Many of them live in the impoverished upland areas, particularly in the north of the country, and have limited access to social services such as education and health. Some groups, among them the Hmong, also frequently encounter suspicion from the administration, even if legal discrimination does not seem to be a problem.

The Commission is aware of reports of clashes between Laotian 'security forces' and some armed Hmong groups. The Commission is also aware of recent reports on the living conditions of these particular groups.

The Commission has stated on several occasions that it is concerned about the human rights situation in Laos and that it will examine these questions in view of the next meeting of the EC-Lao People’s Democratic Republic Joint Committee. During this meeting, the Commission will have the possibility of raising the difficulties faced by ethnic minority groups.

At this stage the Commission has not been invited to take on a role as mediator between particular Hmong groups and the Government of Lao PDR. The Commission will, however, continue to make use of all instruments available in the framework of its political dialogue with Laos to raise issues of concern.

As regards the idea of receiving 2.000 Hmongs into the Union, this is a topic to be discussed with the Member States in the appropriate fora.

 

Question no 77 by Neil MacCormick (H-0493/03)
 Subject: Distortion in video and DVD rental industry
 

Is the Commission aware that small retailers engaged in the video and DVD rental industry are experiencing oppressive conduct from Warner Home Video and other big players in the industry? Will the Commission consider an inquiry into pricing policies that tend to squeeze out small retailers from this business?

 
  
 

(EN) The Commission is aware of the difficulties faced by video rental companies in the United Kingdom caused by the fact that some film companies have ceased to give video rental companies a privileged rental window prior to the retail sale of the same film. The Commission understands that this is exposing such companies to increased competition from retail selling of the films which may constrain the rental revenues of such films.

On the basis of the indications provided by the honourable Member, it would seem that the effects of the alleged restrictive practice are likely to be felt essentially within the United Kingdom market. This would imply that, in the interest of an efficient enforcement of competition rules, the United Kingdom competition authorities would seem to be better placed to deal with such a case.

In this respect, the Commission cannot but take note that the Office of Fair Trading has already been informed of the issues raised in the letter of the honourable Member. At this stage, it has no elements in its possession that would require it to intervene in this matter.

 

Question no 78 by Rodi Kratsa-Tsagaropoulou (H-0495/03)
 Subject: Development deficit of Greek island regions
 

The recent Commission report entitled ‘Analysis of the island regions and outermost regions of the European Union’(1) classifies Greek islands on the basis of statistical data in the very last position. The Ionian islands in particular are classified last of all European island regions as regards per capita income and growth rate. In the sectors of economic structures, access to services, demographic situation and the environment they are presented as being disadvantaged, although Greek statistics give a different impression(2).

On the basis of which individual quantitative and qualitative elements and data is this specific region presented as disadvantaged in this way? Does the Commission have other comparative data (in addition to those referred to in the study) regarding the economy, infrastructures, services, human resources and other local features of the Ionian islands?

Will account be taken of this situation in the interim review of the Third CSF (Community Support Framework)? Does it intend, on the basis of this study on island regions, to promote practical initiatives and measures to support the most backward regions?

 
  
 

(FR) The study on island and outermost regions was carried out during 2002. It was based on a broad range of statistical databases, including the regional databases of NewCronos/Eurostat and various national and local sources.

As far as the Ionian islands are concerned, the Greek National Statistics Office was contacted and supplied a number of variables which are recorded in the database containing all the quantitative information provided by the study.

During the course of the study, several other Greek sources were contacted, mainly the Greek meteorological service, the Ministry of the Environment, the Ministry of the Marine and the University of the Aegean Sea. As far as per capita gross domestic product (GDP) is concerned, the results of the study appear to be reliable, although the data available only cover a NUTS II and III regional level. The regions do not always coincide with the archipelago or the individual islands.

This study was a first attempt to study the island regions and territories in a harmonised manner at European level. It allowed very diverse situations to be identified in these territories and also highlighted the lack of harmonised data.

The conclusions of the study form part of numerous sources of information which will help to give the Commission food for thought on future cohesion policy when the third report on cohesion is prepared.

 
 

(1) 2000. CE 16. O.AT. 118
(2) Latest statistics of the Greek National Statistics Service (ESGE)

 

Question no 79 by Jan Dhaene (H-0499/03)
 Subject: Cruise control in lorries
 

On various occasions in recent months, the E17 Antwerp-Ghent-Lille motorway has been the scene of incredible accidents involving lorry drivers. Lorries are gradually being fitted with cruise control equipment. Inattentive drivers are driving at top speed into the back of traffic jams caused by road works.

This is not an isolated problem. Throughout Europe there are lorry and car accidents caused by cruise control. Such accidents cost lives. Over the last few weeks in Belgium alone, six people have died and 13 have been seriously injured in accidents involving cruise control.

Transport trade unions, too, consider that cruise control does their driver members more harm than good.

Is the Commission sufficiently well informed about the road safety impact of cruise control in lorries?

Is the Commission considering a cruise control ban for lorries? If so, on what timescale does it think that a ban can be established? Are Member States allowed to introduce such a ban independently without changes to European legislation in this area?

 
  
 

(FR) The honourable Member is invited to refer to the reply to written question E-1501/03(1) by Mrs Van Brempt on the same subject. The Commission is concerned by the increase in accidents involving lorries, where the use of speed regulators appears to play a part. It is trying to collect objective information from the Belgian authorities which will enable it to evaluate any possible measures needed.

The Commission does not have any convincing data on the impact on road safety of speed regulators in lorries. On the contrary, studies – including in the Netherlands – have demonstrated that these regulators reduce fuel consumption by between 4 and 10%. They also help responsible drivers to keep to the speed limit.

As with other equipment in vehicles, it is clear that speed regulators require responsible use on the part of drivers. For example, it is not advisable to use these regulators in heavy or unstable traffic or in bad weather conditions. Nor does the Commission plan to ban the general use of speed regulators in lorries. It feels it is more important to improve the training of professional drivers, on which a directive was adopted by Parliament and the Council recently, and control of driving and rest times via the digital tachograph to be introduced in a year's time and via a larger number of controls, which are being planned within the framework of the next review of Directive 88/599(2). The Member States have the right to ban the use of these regulators locally under adverse conditions, such as road works for example.

 
 

(1) OJ L
(2) Council Directive 88/599/EEC of 23 November 1988 on standard checking procedures for the implementation of Regulation (EEC) No 3820/85 on the harmonization of certain social legislation relating to road transport and Regulation (EEC) No 3821/85 on recording equipment in road transport, OJ L 325 of 29 November 1988

 

Question no 80 by Konstantinos Hatzidakis (H-0500/03)
 Subject: Final payments under the 2nd Community Support Framework in Greece
 

What progress has been made in regard to the final payments under the 2nd CSF in Greece since last May (which programmes have been 'closed', which appropriations have been used or lost definitively and what is the final date for the closure of the 2nd CSF for Greece)?

 
  
 

(FR) The Commission has already given the honourable Member numerous items of information in reply to Oral Question H-0255/03.

This reply can be updated on the following points.

As far as the European Regional Development Fund is concerned, the ‘Urban Development – Athens Metro’ programme has been closed. The corresponding payment made to Greece totalled EUR 48.525 million.

The Commission has also informed the Greek authorities of the result of the examination concerning the European Regional Development Fund for all other programmes, to which the Greek authorities have been invited to respond by 30 September 2003 at the latest. As for the programmes concerned by the European Agriculture Guidance and Guarantee Fund (EAGGF), Guidance Section, and the Financial Instrument for Fisheries Guidance (FIFG), an examination of demands for payment is under way.

As far as the European Social Fund is concerned, by the end of April 2003, a total of EUR 173.6 million had been paid under three operational programmes, namely ‘Continued Training'’, ‘Public Administration’, and ‘Education’.

Since May 2003, final payments totalling EUR 37 321 827 for the European Social Fund relating to seven more operational programmes have been made: ‘Research and Technology’, ‘Tourism’, ‘Technical Assistance’, ‘Thessalia’, ‘Ionian Islands’, ‘Northern Aegean’ and ‘Southern Aegean’. The remaining programmes concerned by the European Social Fund are being processed and their closure is foreseen in the next few months.

 

Question no 81 by Efstratios Korakas (H-0506/03)
 Subject: The Peloponnese, the poorest region in Europe
 

According to the new ranking of EU regions published by Eurostat, six of the ten poorest regions in the EU are located in Greece. In half of these regions there has been a decline in the per capita GDP; this is true of the Peloponnese which has a per capita GDP of only 56 units.

Will the Commission say whether it considers that measures to reduce regional disparities, in particular in respect of Greece, are inadequate, and how it can explain that the poorest Greek regions are diverging from, instead of converging with, the Community average, in particular the Peloponnese which is continuing to decline, and is becoming the poorest region in Europe?

 
  
 

(EN) The honourable Member is informed that increased efforts are made through the Community Support Framework 2000-2006 for Greece in order to produce a better convergence of Greek regions to the Community average:

- The procedures of project selection have been extensively improved, which in turn is expected to improve the quality of projects co-funded through the Community Structural Funds (CSF) III.

- Cost effectiveness has been introduced in the procedure of project selection, a fact that in turn is expected to secure value for money for projects co-funded through the CSF III.

- Increased technical assistance is available for some final beneficiaries, such as municipalities.

- Through the CSF III strategy, the Greek government recognises the need to support the productive potential of regions not only through investment on infrastructure, but also through investment in human resources. The part of CSF III directed to human resources is presently about 20% (in CSF II it was 15%), and it is quite possible that this percentage could be increased on the occasion of the mid-term review.

As regards the Peloponnese, adding to the above, it should be noted that the new University of Peloponnese is expected to increase the human potential of the region.

 

Question no 82 by Jillian Evans (H-0509/03)
 Subject: Miscarriages of justice
 

Several legal cases involving malpractices and resulting sometimes in lengthy wrongful imprisonment give rise to concern regarding the efficiency and probity of some police officers, current and retired, of the South Wales Force from the 1980s to the present day.

Those who have had their freedom violated in this way have asked the UK Government to hold an independent public inquiry into this matter. Could refusal constitute a breach of Article 6(1) of the Treaty on European Union or of Chapter VI, Article II-47 of the Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union when it is expected to become legally binding next year?

 
  
 

(FR) The Commission does not have any information on the cases to which the honourable Member refers.

It is nevertheless clear from the information provided in her question that it falls to the UK authorities to ensure that basic personal rights, including the rights of the accused in criminal proceedings, are respected, and to take all necessary measures to this end.

Refusal by the UK authorities to hold an independent public enquiry requested by the victims would only fall under European law and constitute a breach of Article 6(1) of the Treaty on European Union if:

this breach could be viewed as 'serious and persistent' within the meaning of Article 7 of that Treaty was assessed as presenting a 'clear risk of a serious breach' under that Article; or

his breach were to arise in connection with the implementation of EU law by the United Kingdom.

It appears to the Commission that neither of these situations apply in the case in point.

The Commission would nonetheless draw the honourable Member's attention to the fact that it is aware of the need to ensure effective protection of procedural safeguards for suspects and defendants in criminal proceedings in the European Union, and has presented a Green Paper on this subject with a view to stimulating ideas that could culminate in a legislative initiative in this area(1).

Finally, it should be emphasised that anyone who considers their basic rights to have been violated has the possibility of recourse before the European Court of Human Rights, after all internal means have been exhausted.

 
 

(1) Green Paper on procedural safeguards for suspects and defendants in criminal proceedings throughout the European Union - COM(2003) 75 final.

 

Question no 83 by Ioannis Souladakis (H-0511/03)
 Subject: White Paper on Transport and motorways of the seas
 

As part of the review of the Transeuropean Networks, is the Commission considering extending them to include the motorways of the seas of the enlargement countries (Cyprus, Malta and the Baltic countries: Lithuania, Latvia and Estonia), of the EU regions bordering on the enlargement countries and the countries of the Mediterranean basin?

 
  
 

(FR) The review of the guidelines for the development of the trans-European transport network which the Commission is currently preparing will include the networks of the new Member States, so that the revised text takes effect in the new Member States when they actually join the Community.

The sea motorways have been recommended as priority projects by the High-Level Group instructed by the Commission to draw up recommendations on new priority projects in trans-European networks. The countries quoted, as well as other Community countries, are in the zones identified by the Group: Baltic Sea Motorway, Western European Sea Motorway, South-Eastern European Sea Motorway, South-Western European Sea Motorway(1).

As far as the countries of the Mediterranean basin are concerned, the importance of developing good connections was clearly recognised in the communication on the development of a Euro-Mediterranean transport network(2). The communication lists the conditions and actions needed prior to the realisation of a Euro-Mediterranean transport network; the importance of sea motorways is also highlighted in this context.

 
 

(1) - Baltic Sea Motorway (linking the Member States on the Baltic Sea to the Member States in Central and Western Europe)
- Western European Sea Motorway (linking the Iberian peninsular to the North Sea and the Irish Sea via the Atlantic Arc)
- South-Eastern European Sea Motorway (linking the Adriatic Sea to the Ionian Sea and the Eastern Mediterranean in order to include Cyprus)
- South-Western European Sea Motorway (Western Mediterranean), linking Spain, France, Italy, including Malta, and linking the South-Eastern European Sea Motorway
(2) Commission Communication to the Council and the European Parliament on the development of a Euro-Mediterranean transport network (COM (2003)376)

 

Question no 84 by Ioannis Patakis (H-0513/03)
 Subject: Erosion of savings of ordinary people
 

The constant reduction of interest rates on deposits with commercial banks - which currently range in Greece from the negative to 0.5% on savings of up to € 60 000, while inflation is running at over 3.8% - is slashing incomes and savings, particularly those of ordinary people. At the same time, the banks are multiplying their profits by charging interest rates of more than 11% on overdrafts and a number of other products, while big business receives preferential treatment through the possibility of investing in public and private securities which yield far greater rates of interest than savings accounts but are not available to the general public.

Will the Commission take steps to protect the savings of ordinary people, eliminate discriminatory access to investment products and put an end to big business preying on the capital of small savers and investors?

 
  
 

(EN) The Commission thanks the honourable Member for its question concerning the erosion of savings of ordinary people in Greece.

At the outset the Commission would like to recall that, within a context of heightened financial market integration in the Union, a common monetary policy and a single currency in the euro area, market conditions for financial institutions in Member States are increasingly competitive. However, individual financial institutions, whilst being influenced by common factors such as a change in the market borrowing/lending rate, may still decide the conditions applied to their customers according to their own individual strategy, balance sheet and other considerations. For example, in times of weak economic growth, financial institutions may decide to give more weight to risk considerations than otherwise.

Investment in securities bring the potential for greater return than cash deposits but this is in exchange for greater risk to capital, so care needs to be taken in comparing the returns available from deposit accounts and securities. As regards the situation in Greece that the honourable Member refers to, the Commission is currently not aware of any discriminatory access to investment products for Greek citizens, although the Commission has in the past dealt with a case of fiscal discrimination of the income derived from investment funds. This case was investigated by the Commission and, following the commitment of the Greek authorities to rectify the situation, was closed in 2002. Should any additional and more precise information be brought to the Commission's attention, it will investigate the matter further.

The Commission has consulted the Greek Competition Authority on the existence of a collusion or an abuse of a collective dominant position by financial institutions in Greece. The Greek Competition Authority does not have any significant indications that can lead to the suspicion of an infringement of competition law but it will keep a close look at the relevant market and will conduct an investigation in case there is a behaviour that can possibly harm competition.

In addition, at this stage, from the Commission market monitoring experience, there are no concrete indications of an infringement of articles 81 or 82 of the EC Treaty. However, the Commission would reconsider this conclusion if it becomes aware of concrete evidence of a competition law infringement.

 

Question no 85 by Konstantinos Alyssandrakis (H-0514/03)
 Subject: Measures to combat child pornography on the Internet
 

The distribution of child pornography over the Internet is assuming alarming proportions and is even affecting countries such as Greece where the percentage of the population with Internet access is low. Recently in Volos, a network was uncovered illegally distributing photographic and video material via a website with 5000 members and over 600 000 visitors. Some estimates put the number of similar sites in Europe at over 10 000.

Will the Commission take the necessary measures to prosecute electronic crime and, in particular, child pornography effectively, which will target providers and users alike, protect the victims, combat the degrading of human life and support the services and NGOs fighting this inhuman crime?

 
  
 

(EN) The Commission fully shares the concerns expressed by the honourable Member of Parliament about the growing problem of child pornography distributed through the Internet. It believes there is need for continuous efforts to combat harmful and illegal content on the Internet through international co-operation amongst governments, particularly law enforcement and judicial authorities, but also between governments and the Internet industry, dedicated hotlines and non-governmental organisations to effectively tackle this horrifying phenomenon.

Primary responsibility for dealing with illegal content (including child pornography) lies with the appropriate law enforcement and judicial authorities of the Member States, which co-operate internationally in the fight against child pornography on the Internet through the existing channels of communications, such as Europol and Interpol.

However, the Union has been a forerunner in the fight against illegal and harmful content since 1996, thanks to the joint approach adopted by the Parliament and the Council. The Safer Internet Action Plan adopted by the Council and the Parliament in 1999 (1) is a major element in the Commission’s activity in this field. It provides funding for a European network of hotlines allowing users to report illegal content, including child pornography. In March 2002 (2), on the basis of a Commission proposal, the current Action Plan was extended for a second phase of two years, in line with a review that took account of past experience and new technologies, so as to ensure co-ordination with parallel work in the field of network and information security.

The Union strategy to combat child pornography also consists of legal instruments and practical measures against computer crime and child pornography. These include the Commission proposal for a Council Framework Decision (3) on approximation of laws and sanctions in the field of sexual exploitation of children, with particular reference to child pornography on the Internet (4), which the Commission hopes the Council will be able to adopt formally in the very near future, and the Council Recommendation of September 1998 (5) on the Protection of Minors and Human Dignity and the Council Decision of May 2000 to combat child pornography on the Internet (6).

As an example, at the beginning of 2003, the Commission received the final version of the 'International Child Exploitation Database' feasibility study, which was co-funded under the STOP II Programme and run by a project group composed by experts from several Member States. The project group has issued various recommendations, highlighting the need for introducing a sophisticated networked international child sexual exploitation image database, building on the fledgling system at Interpol. Such a database, would have to take into account the different national laws governing images of child sexual exploitation and protection of personal data.

In March 2003, the Commission received an application by several Member States, Europol and other third Countries under the AGIS programme for funding an implementation study as a concrete follow-up to a feasibility study concluded in December 2002. Applications are now being evaluated according to the rules of the funding programme. The final funding decision will be announced by autumn.

Additionally, the Safer Internet Programme provides funding for activities aimed at combating illegal and harmful content, as part of a coherent approach by the European Union. ‘Safer Internet’ aims at:

ensuring more extensive interfacing with programmes and actions conducted at national level,

improving exchange of information and best practice between Member States,

developing software enabling parents and/or teachers to limit children’s access to inappropriate material and content on the Internet,

offering dedicated web services which provide safe access to the Internet.

 
 

(1) Decision no 276/1999/EC OJ L33, 6.2.1999 as amended by decision No 1151/2003/EC OJ L 162 1.7.2003
(2) COM (2002) 152 final
(3) COM (2002) 854 final
(4) The Council reached a common approach on this Commission proposal on 14 October 2002. Two Member States have still parliamentary reservations on the proposal.
(5) OJ L 270, 7.10.98
(6) OJ L 138, 9.6.2000
